        <tr id="so9" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>While Anderson climbed through the minor leagues, he steadily cut his strikeouts down from an alarming level to a merely poor one. That it jumped in his first taste of the bigs, back up to about 27%, should surprise no one. Now comes the adjustment period. If Anderson is capable of sufficiently cutting down on his strikeouts at the major league level, that means more balls in play, more base hits, and most importantly, more stolen-base opportunities and runs. Therefore, it's encouraging that his 15-game rolling average swinging-strike and chase rates both plummeted at the end of the year. Anderson hit nine homers in 2016, but given the league-wide power surge, that's hardly anything to write home about these days. Anderson's value is  tied primarily to his legs and lineup. He's also never shown any semblance of plate discipline in the minors. He's a slap hitter reliant on a ton of ground balls in order to reach first and wreak havoc. Hitting at the top of the order is good for racking up runs, and there are worse players to have hitting behind you than Jose Abreu and Todd Frazier. That said, he shouldn't be anyone's first option in standard leagues.  (Rylan Edwards)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Until he proves otherwise, Anderson is a one-dimensional player lost in an increasingly deep position. He brings value with his legs but is most appropriate in deep and AL-only leagues that don't count walks.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
